B007CW2FCA|5.0|To commemorate the 200th anniversary of Beethoven's birth in 1970, High Fidelity magazine published a comprehensive survey, in book form, of the composer's recordings that were available at the time. Their distinguished piano critic, Harris Goldsmith, wrote the sections on the piano music and concertos, and his opinions and insights are still valuable today. Of the Arrau cycle of the 32 sonatas, Goldsmith wrote the following:<br /><br />\\"Following Arrau through the sonatas was a stimulating, sometimes perplexing experience: stimulating because of the pianist's mastery and profound insight into the music; perplexing because although I am a confirmed adherent to an opposite musical polarity (e.g., the Schnabel/Toscanini axis) I kept finding myself fascinated and drawn into Arrau's interpretations in spite of myself. Granted, I look for metrical severity and Arrau's inflected phrasing is sometimes stretched to theoretical limits; I also gravitate toward fast tempos and headstrong brio, while Arrau's approach more often than not is leisurely and reasoned. But this is not the clich&eacute;-ridden self-indulgence of a typical stock-in-trade 'Romantic' pianist: there is vast scholarship and harmonic analysis behind Arrau's seeming arbitrariness and it always manages to clarify some aspect of the writing. The pianist is helped, of course, by a superbly finished technique and a fabulous tone - solid, velvety rich, full of glint and gleaming color. Chords are luscious and well balanced, his scales are even and caressing. Arrau takes care to reveal inner melodic lines; he is scrupulous about observing rests, and unlike so many players, always begins his appoggiaturas on (rather than before) the beat. He is, I might add, the only artist to observe every repeat (which one might consider a mixed blessing). These details are important, of course, but ought not cloud the real point of Arrau's music-making which provides a deeply expressive, subjective thesis about Beethoven's music.\\" (\\"The Recordings of Beethoven as Viewed by the Critics of High Fidelity,\\" 1971)<br /><br />The \\"subjective thesis\\" observed by Goldsmith was expressed by Arrau himself in an illuminating essay that accompanied the Philips box set of the pianist's complete sonata cycle in 1971:<br /><br />\\"Like all cultural heroes, Beethoven exemplifies in his creative output all the spiritual and psychic battles of the mythic hero - the hero who is given superhuman tasks to overcome and who after untold struggles (truly bloodied but undaunted) emerges the victor and, as in the case of the greatest creative giants (Shakespeare, Michelangelo, Rembrandt, Goethe) finally attains the highest state of self-realization and illumination. This is especially true of Beethoven, who struggled creatively possibly on a more titanic scale than even Michelangelo - in the sense that he wrought his works into shape with an inexhaustible inner sense and vision of perfection and completion. In the end he reached a mystical union with the godhead, as it were, and on a higher plane of transcendence than almost anyone else in the history of Western art.\\" (\\"Thoughts on Beethoven and the Piano Sonatas\\")<br /><br />This outlook limns Arrau's interpretations of the Beethoven sonatas. My own point of view after having lived with these recordings for over forty years is that Arrau's genius, diverse as it was given his masterful interpretations of Liszt, Brahms, Debussy, and Schumann, revealed itself most completely in the music of Beethoven, especially those works (and there were many) with which he fully identified. The supreme statements of Arrau's Beethoven can be heard in the Appassionata, Funeral March, Pastoral, Les Adieux, Waldstein, Moonlight and Tempest sonatas, as well as the five late sonatas (opp. 101, 106, 109, 110 and 111). He also turned in exceptional performances of opp. 2, no.3; 10, no. 3; 27, no. 1 (Sonata Quasi una Fantasia); 54; 78 and 90 as well as the Eroica and C Minor variations. Infact, his playing and insights are on such a consistently high level that out of the 32 sonatas, Arrau hardly registered a \\"miss.\\"<br /><br />Of the last five sonatas Arrau pondered \\"Where did all of that come from? [How did Beethoven] enter spheres of such transcendent meaning as were never encountered in music before or, in all truth, since? Who could foretell such a span of evolution which sees him create at the end what can only be called a metaphysical language of music...where trills become a trembling of the soul and arpeggios reach out into the infinite altogether.... In Op. 111...Beethoven reaches into cosmic spaces which open up into infinitude, into a state of mystical rapture which Goethe called 'der Fall nach oben' - the fall upward....\\"<br /><br />Given this level of perception, it's no wonder that Arrau's readings of the triple opus numbered sonatas uniquely penetrate the inner dimension of this music. While not everyone approved of his approach to late Beethoven (Harris Goldsmith called some of the interpretations too rhetorical and \\"Furtwanglerish\\"), I think these documents reign supreme in the canon of Beethoven recordings.<br /><br />After decades of listening to Beethoven including the cycles set down by Schnabel, Kempff, Brendel, Frank, and Backhaus among others, it is Arrau whose recordings are the most consistently insightful and compelling, not to mention the most technically polished. This reissue set on the Decca label deserves a place in every music library.<br /><br />Addendum: For a perspective of Arrau at work, I highly recommend a recently issued 2-dvd set of the pianist's appearances at the 1970 and 1977 Beethovenfest in Bonn: [[ASIN:B004TWOXI0 Claudio Arrau: Beethoven Piano Sonatas]]. Between the two recitals Arrau plays eight Beethoven sonatas (op. 2, no. 3; op. 27 no. 1; op. 27, no. 2; op. 53; op. 57; op. 81a; op. 109; and op. 111). These are unforgettable documents that capture the pianist at his titanic best.<br /><br />Addendum II:<br /><br />There have been a number of comments regarding the sonics on this set, so I thought I would add my own to the mix. The previous Philips box set, which was issued in 1998, offered 24 bit sound and I can't say that it was much of an improvement over the original cd set except for the elimination of intrusive electronic clicks that some mistakenly believed were Arrau's fingernails striking the keys (as it happens, his nails were trimmed to the nub). There's only so much that can be done with fifty year old source material and it wouldn't surprise me if maximum remediation had already been done by the engineers at Philips.<br /><br />Having said that, with the exception of the above-mentioned electronic noise in the original cd box set which has been corrected, I've never found the sound picture to be \\"claustrophobic\\" or \\"boxy\\" - descriptions that have been repeated numerous times over the years. To the contrary, I think this set projects a good deal of Arrau's unique sound - the richness, the color, the organ-like fullness and warmth. The sound picture, however, is not completely consistent. First of all, Arrau began his cycle by recording sonatas nos. 11-15 in 1962, and these are the only ones I find to be sonically challenged. They sound thin and rather strained, but they're still way ahead of what EMI's engineers were producing for Arrau in 1960 (an example of which is [[ASIN:B0002VEQG6 Chopin: Piano Sonata No. 3; Fantasy]]). Sixteen months later, he recorded the Pathetique, Waldstein and Hammerklavier sonatas and followed this with a batch of early sonatas (nos. 1-7) in 1964. The rest were recorded over various sessions in 1965 and 1966. The point of all this is that while I tend to listen to the sonatas in chronological order, they were not recorded that way; however, the sound, never less than acceptable, definitely improves along the way - even though it may seem variable if you listen chronologically.fact, his playing and insights are on such a consistently high level that out of the 32 sonatas, Arrau hardly registered a \\"miss.\\"<br /><br />Of the last five sonatas Arrau pondered \\"Where did all of that come from? [How did Beethoven] enter spheres of such transcendent meaning as were never encountered in music before or, in all truth, since? Who could foretell such a span of evolution which sees him create at the end what can only be called a metaphysical language of music...where trills become a trembling of the soul and arpeggios reach out into the infinite altogether.... In Op. 111...Beethoven reaches into cosmic spaces which open up into infinitude, into a state of mystical rapture which Goethe called 'der Fall nach oben' - the fall upward....\\"<br /><br />Given this level of perception, it's no wonder that Arrau's readings of the triple opus numbered sonatas uniquely penetrate the inner dimension of this music. While not everyone approved of his approach to late Beethoven (Harris Goldsmith called some of the interpretations too rhetorical and \\"Furtwanglerish\\"), I think these documents reign supreme in the canon of Beethoven recordings.<br /><br />After decades of listening to Beethoven including the cycles set down by Schnabel, Kempff, Brendel, Frank, and Backhaus among others, it is Arrau whose recordings are the most consistently insightful and compelling, not to mention the most technically polished. This reissue set on the Decca label deserves a place in every music library.<br /><br />Addendum: For a perspective of Arrau at work, I highly recommend a recently issued 2-dvd set of the pianist's appearances at the 1970 and 1977 Beethovenfest in Bonn: [[ASIN:B004TWOXI0 Claudio Arrau: Beethoven Piano Sonatas]]. Between the two recitals Arrau plays eight Beethoven sonatas (op. 2, no. 3; op. 27 no. 1; op. 27, no. 2; op. 53; op. 57; op. 81a; op. 109; and op. 111). These are unforgettable documents that capture the pianist at his titanic best.<br /><br />AddendumII:<br /><br />There have been a number of comments regarding the sonics on this set, so I thought I would add my own to the mix. The previous Philips box set, which was issued in 1998, offered 24 bit sound and I can't say that it was much of an improvement over the original cd set except for the elimination of intrusive electronic clicks that some mistakenly believed were Arrau's fingernails striking the keys (as it happens, his nails were trimmed to the nub). There's only so much that can be done with fifty year old source material and it wouldn't surprise me if maximum remediation had already been done by the engineers at Philips.<br /><br />Having said that, with the exception of the above-mentioned electronic noise in the original cd box set which has been corrected, I've never found the sound picture to be \\"claustrophobic\\" or \\"boxy\\" - descriptions that have been repeated numerous times over the years. To the contrary, I think this set projects a good deal of Arrau's unique sound - the richness, the color, the organ-like fullness and warmth. The sound picture, however, is not completely consistent. First of all, Arrau began his cycle by recording sonatas nos. 11-15 in 1962, and these are the only ones I find to be sonically challenged. They sound thin and rather strained, but they're still way ahead of what EMI's engineers were producing for Arrau in 1960 (an example of which is [[ASIN:B0002VEQG6 Chopin: Piano Sonata No. 3; Fantasy]]). Sixteen months later, he recorded the Pathetique, Waldstein and Hammerklavier sonatas and followed this with a batch of early sonatas (nos. 1-7) in 1964. The rest were recorded over various sessions in 1965 and 1966. The point of all this is that while I tend to listen to the sonatas in chronological order, they were not recorded that way; however, the sound, never less than acceptable, definitely improves along the way - even though it may seem variable if you listen chronologically.

And was the whole school play thing filler? That went for, like, 200-300 pages alone! [!]\\").  I wasn't convinced how fast that love thang happened.<br /><br />I see a lot of \\"wow, what an ending\\" \\"he did it right\\" type fare here in the review forum for \\"11/22/63\\". I know King rides with Shakespeare in being able to deliver, well, tragic endings and be able to retain a popular fanbase . . . but, the whole \\"charred/scared/future land even after changing something in the past that SHOULD have resulted in a positive thing\\" when Jake/George saved JFK, I mean, eek, King.  No, King, no. If I gotta stand alone and say, \\"no, sir . . . JFK surviving an assassination THAT cunning, by a Communist sympathizer. No, no way would things sour\\" or at least it SHOULDN'T have been when you can do all kinds of things w/a fictional platform, then, I'll be a Toshiro Mifune standing in the middle of town hea. There needed to be more of a richer conclusion when Jake came back to 2011 after saving JFK.<br /><br />And I'm not talking that NO tragic situation should've or would've \\"tangented\\" from a surviving JFK; but, mega EARTHQUAKES???? A few days AFTER JFK survives? \\"The past is obdurate\\" . . . we're told that throughout in this book.  But, I also kept thinking pasts are DIFFERENT in each string.  So, in THAT timeline string -- which means that JFK survives -- why would time be so angry as to create earthquakes and have JFK suffer in his 2nd term disproportionate to his aims? King STILL coulda' had George/Jake and Sadie dance in that future (which happened, yes?  I simply cringed at the seeming non-King-ish ending of Sadie giving an \\"I remember you from somewhere\\" which is par for the course with love un-united American/time-travel style.  I mean, c'mon.  Is this the Oxygen channel sci-fi'/time-travel offering of the year, or what?  King mentions that his son helps with the ending.  I think dad should stick to his own instincts if Jr. had a hand in coming up with the man-from-the-future dances with the she-grey-haired social conscious). I mean, give some hardcore -- he makes gov'ment agents look bad in \\"Firestarter\\", so, where was the CIA being dismantled in a JFK second term, or similar ilk, you gotta wonder? Would've been in line w/Stephen King's apparent politics (to which he rightfully has a right to, I'm not complaining about that, make no mistake [this will be important to keep in mind for the next paragraph]).<br /><br />Anybody confused with the Afterward, too? Through the whole book, and even in a few King interviews I caught (and King says this in the afterward, too, adding a \\"Tabitha's the\\" one who takes note of the conspiracy theories of events 11/22/1963 underscore), he's convinced Oswald acted alone, but, at the same time, adding that the collective hate of Confederate types guided Oswald's bullet's what I got from King's hint when he mentioned the infamous Zapruder frame.<br /><br />One thing that was mentioned . . . and this one just needs to be re-written for the paperback, yes, indeed (as I've seen mentioned in one or two or more message boards discussing the book), how the heck does Al suddenly have a visiting nurse to his house alla' sudden after the two minutes of Jake/George's first foray into 1958. And if you say, \\"well, Jake/George initial `going into 1958' helped provide that nurse,\\" then, why isn't Al a totally different person altogether? Would he even remember Jake/George?<br /><br />I mean, I don't get the mistakes Jake/George kept doing. Singing a \\"prior-to-existence\\" Rolling Stones song in the car when another song is playing on the radio (lemme know if I'm wrong here)? How could he make that mistake when he's well into \\"I am a time-traveller mode\\"? I dunno . . .<br /><br />The school play situation was simply a busted firecracker dud in the middle of it all. It could've been a separate novel or short story altogether. Methinks a horde of football players finding giddiness in acting in a school play creates a decent butterfly effect and/or watershed moment, no?<br /><br />I think I understood the whole \\"watershed\\"-events-when-it-comes-to-time-travel issue brought up in the book. I don't think it NEEDS to have such an impact (certainly not leading to magical earthquakes) when such events are prevented. If 09/11 never happened, 09/12's main headline would be something about Brangelina or whatever, you know? Victims of 09/11 would've lived and life would've gone on minus TSA. Think about it. And, heck, to me, we still woulda' gone after Saddam, Ahmadinejad, and Kim Jong Il. I mean, regardless of 09/11, their dreams of \\"nuclear\\" hung around them like bad henchmen; they didn't suddenly come up with such plans after the events of 09/11/2001, we can agree (or should).  Situations w/those three were coming to a head and going into the 21st century, they are threats.  And they would've been handled regardless.<br /><br />To me, writers of time-travel fiction need to get a better handle on that untamed bull on the butterfly effect (sorry to mix metaphors here). Describe the butterfly effect to your readers only once and really DO something with it, you know? Impress us. Don't auto-Orwellian a conclusion. Guess what? A butterfly effect happens as easily as I (and you) decide to have a glass of water instead of a can of coke. And every other action we do, especially legally and normally, in our day-to-day creates a butterfly effect.  Hitler's rejection as an artist, what?  Would've never been known had he went to work in a bakery and died of old age instead.  Right?<br /><br />I would tell King that \\"11/22/63\\"'s happy ending shouldn't ONLY have been about Jake/George and Sadie. Yes, yes, the 11/22/1963s and the 09/11/2001s happen in our world and, for those of us lucky enough (one sect) or by the grace of God (the rest of us) not to have any direct tragedy with such situations, well, we simply go on, as is needed for humanity to do so after such events, and live the rest of our lives, with our own happinesses, successes, and tragedies. Life goes and there are many butterfly effects. Do you SEE an earthquake every hour, on the hour in your real life? No.<br /><br />To be fair, King's most probably was satisfied with \\"11/22/63\\"'s ending (though, he MAY have had problems having, again, admitted that his son came up with a better ending, am I reading it correctly when S. King says so?).  But, heck, where was the KING ending, you know?  Who would've hated on, in a post-assassination attempt setting, JFK's entourage, walkin' `round in government bldg. surroundings in Dallas, and Oswald being carted off , walking `round in government bldg. settings, crossing paths in the chaos and JFK slapping him around when they got too close?  Who would've hated on Oswald, post-assassination attempt, asking to go to the restroom and Pennywise appearing in the toilet and eating Oswald up???  Sounds silly.  But, methinks the \\"King ending\\" wasn't there ('specially, sorry to reiterate, that last Jake/George and Sadie dance. Whatever happened to those fellas anyways?]).<br /><br />\\"11/22/63\\" needed to have an ending that made those of us who wished that JFK live raise our fist when reading King's latest with a verbal and loud \\"right on!!!\\" the same way we did when Paul Sheldon gave Annie Wilkes her comeuppance. B005I4JG80|5.0|Druckerman's work is insightful, well-written, and fun. If you appreciate cultural comparisons (especially linguistic ones), you will enjoy this book. Her writing is engaging and smart, and I found myself fully captivated by the book. The book is, as the title indicates, primarily about the baby years.  Through an expertly woven set of scenes, the book follows the stages of Druckerman's first born up through age 6. One of her concluding thoughts is this: \\"[French parenting] mostly requires a parent to shift how she conceives of her relationship to her children and what she expects from them.\\"  If you like this book, or are interested in more about her final point, you may want to read David Code's book:[[ASIN:0824525388 To Raise Happy Kids, Put Your Marriage First]]. Code, like Druckerman, lived in France and bases much of his work on the cultural comparisons he gleaned from his time there. (Code also lived in Japan.) Code's book, unlike Druckerman's, focuses on preserving the marriage and is equally applicable to families with older children. I highly recommend both books!

It shows the extremes of the city from the unwavering love and loyalty of the Indian people, to the depth of the corruption and power that ultimately runs the city, as well as showing what life in the slum would be like compared to the rich who live there. If I ever did go to India one day I would be going with my eyes slightly more open to my surroundings than they were previously.<br /><br />The characters were complex and vivid and even the most despicable characters displayed some endearing quality. B007BVOF8E|1.0|The only good thing about this waste of celluloid is that it may stimulate interest in the excellent book and TV series -- the novel by John le Carre, volume one of his Search for Karla trilogy, and the BBC 6-hour miniseries starring Alec Guinness as George Smiley.  In fact, if you haven't already encountered those predecessors, your chances of making heads or tails of this new effort are slim.<br /><br />Other reviewers have covered the stone-faced acting of Gary Oldman, the confusing, non-responsive passages of dialog, and the dark, completely uninteresting sets and cinematography.  But the thing that wrecks this movie for me is the loss of any sense of the original story.<br /><br />The novel is a wonderful progression of character and plot that reminds me of Charles Dickens or Jane Austen.  In the book, and the BBC series, the main character arcs -- Jim Prideaux, George Smiley, Peter Guillam, Ricky Tarr -- are mixed with explorations of the supporting actors -- Percy Alleline, Toby Esterhase, Roy Bland, Bill Haydon, Connie Sachs, Jim Roach, Roddy Martindale, Oliver Lacon, Sam Collins, Russian master spy Karla/Guestmann, and especially Jerry Westerby, who becomes the hero of The Honorable Schoolboy, book two of the trilogy -- in a way that leads the reader inexorably to the uncovering of the mole.<br /><br />In the movie there is none of this depth and certainly no enjoyment.  In fact, it's as if the writers sought at every pass to remove elements that reveal the characters and their relationships.  Other reviewers blame the compression into a 2-hour movie, but that just doesn't explain it.  For instance, watching the movie only, you would never guess that Guillam is Smiley's old friend, the person he sought as a drinking companion after being dismissed as assistant head of MI6.  In the car on the way to see Lacon Peter addresses him as Mr. Smiley instead of George.  That was the first discordant note for me.  There were many, many others.<br /><br />Other strikes against the movie include:<br /><br />* Horrible, horrible casting.  While these are obviously professional actors and carry off their roles as written, compare the wonderful casting choices in the BBC series -- tough-guy Ian Bannen as Jim Prideaux, masculine-cum-sardonic Michael Jayston as Peter Guillam, Ian Richardson as supercilious, upper-crust Bill Haydon, the wonderful character actor Joss Ackland as Jerry Westerby.  And of course Alec Guinness, the most gifted actor of all time, as Smiley.<br /><br />* Unnecessary changes to the story line that add nothing, make no sense, and are ineptly done.  Perhaps combining Sam Collins with Jerry Westerby saved time, but then why dress Jerry up in a tuxedo, the way Sam would have been as ex-spy-turned-casino manager?  Never explained.  Why the shooting of Irina in front of Jim Prideaux while a prisoner in the Soviet Union?  They were completely separated in both plot and time.  Jim must have been as confused as I was.<br /><br />* Gary Oldman's portrayal of Smiley.  From movies like Dracula and The Professional we know Oldman is amazing, almost always worth watching.  Here he seems clueless as to his chosen craft.  Smiley, as written and as portrayed by Alec Guinness, was quiet, but commanding and incisive when he spoke, a pivotal character.  Oldman is just silent.<br /><br />* The complete waste of John Hurt as Control -- compare Alexander Knox in the BBC series.<br /><br />* Peter Guillam breaking up with his gay lover -- in the book he is ostentatiously heterosexual.  An obligatory nod to Hollywood's politically correct gay agenda?<br /><br />* Clumsily written scenes -- for example, Alleline's confrontation with Guillam over Tarr's reappearance in England.  Watch the BBC version to see the right way to do this, with little extra investment in time expended.<br /><br />* Poor choice of music, especially at the end.  Bewildering, in fact.<br /><br />* The MI6 office Christmas party.  Nothing remotely like that in thebook.  And why, why, why have Santa Claus in a Lenin mask and everyone happily singing along with the USSR national anthem?  Why??<br /><br />* The Americanization of the dialog by removing le Carre's use of British idiom.  If we Americans really don't know that \\"sacked\\" means fired, are we incapable of figuring it out?<br /><br />* Addition of gratuitous profanity -- the s-word instead of \\"chickenfeed\\" when describing the Witchcraft intelligence material that bamboozled MI6.  Connie Sachs's use of the f-word.  Not in the book and totally unnecessary.  Possibly to gain a PG-13 rating?<br /><br />* Gratuitous violence -- the shooting of Irina, Jim Prideaux using a sniper rifle instead of a karate chop at the end.  I guess American audiences just won't accept a spy movie without gunplay and gore.  I blame Sean Connery and Jason Bourne.<br /><br />(One note of praise:  At least here MI6 has a decent-sized building.  The BBC production gave the impression they were in rented offices over a failing London bookshop.)<br /><br />As this project proceeded, someone must have seen the disaster it was headed for.  I'm surprised le Carre allowed his name to be associated with the final product.  Was there a mole in the production company?<br /><br />So, read the book and watch the TV series.  If those make you a Karla fanatic the way I am, then you may want to watch this hideously deformed descendant just for closure.  Otherwise, I suggest you skip it.

1449305172|3.0|Ash Maurya describes his book as follows:<br /><br />«Running Lean is a handbook for practicing entrepreneurs who want to increase their odds of success.»<br /><br />Which, for me, was originally off-putting. Why? When I was reading the blurb, I focused in on the words \\"innovate\\" and \\"iterate\\" and blocked out \\"venture\\" and \\"bootstrapping\\". I came at this book as an engineer, not as an entrepreneur. And my initial enthusiasm quickly waned: is this going to be one of those self-important business books? But pretty quickly, I figured out that it was not; Maurya was about to lay out a plan for (in essence) applying the scientific method to your business plan.<br /><br />Again, as an engineer by trade, I caught myself thinking: this is not what I came here for, I came here for ideas about effective agile development. That's in there, but it's just one of the many lessons that is embedded in Running Lean. Maurya writes lucidly about how to take your product/business idea and shape it; about how to organize your team to focus on The Problem and The Solution; about how to reach out to customers and interview them, to help them help you define the problem space; about how to pull out real information from those interviews and test releases, and learn real lessons instead of just confirming how great you think you are. The lessons of Running Lean help you to create a real solution for a real problem, and not just be a clever solution looking for the right problem.<br /><br />But what about us engineers? What about folks like me that were looking for strategies on development iteration? Those lessons are in there, too -- if you can get past the entrepreneurese and pay attention to the content.<br /><br />There's a quote in there:<br /><br />«Not only does this approach delay validation of one of the riskier parts of the model (because it's too easy for a user to say yes), but a lack of strong customer \\"commitment\\" can also be detrimental to optimal learning.»<br /><br />And it made me think of a recent post by Gus Mueller (of Flying Meat Software). In that post, Mueller talks about what his historical pricing model has been for VoodooPad (one of his flagship products), and in particular what the pricing model has been for folks on an upgrade path; and then he goes on to talk about how the Mac App Store has disrupted that. In discussing all of this, he breaks down a number of different approaches he could take in the face of this new reality for his business. He is concerned about price, and naturally he is concerned about product quality; but he is also concerned about the relationships that his customers build with his products. Mueller writes:<br /><br />«I can already hear someone saying \\"just charge less and you'll get four times as many customers- which is twice as much money!\\". This will also multiply the support burden (and probably by more than 2x). Lower price means more people will buy a product on a whim. I'd rather have fewer customers that have done the research, compared multiple products, and is willing to pay a higher price for a product that fits their needs.»<br /><br />Fewer but more committed customers. Customers that have done the research. Customers that have decided that this is a product that solves (in a very real way) a very real problem for them. This sentiment dovetailed with Maurya's lessons from Running Lean, and it resonated with me in a big and very important way. In a way, this is exactly the sort of thing that Maurya is writing about -- about not chasing \\"vanity metrics\\" like hits or downloads or installs or sign-ups, but instead focusing on asking hard questions and finding real answers, and ultimately building real solutions for real problems for real people. But make no mistake, what Maurya posits is no easy task -- it requires discipline, and there is a sanguine optimism that underlies his implicit assumption that you can do it.d it made me think of a recent post by Gus Mueller (of Flying Meat Software). In that post, Mueller talks about what his historical pricing model has been for VoodooPad (one of his flagship products), and in particular what the pricing model has been for folks on an upgrade path; and then he goes on to talk about how the Mac App Store has disrupted that. I'd rather have fewer customers that have done the research, compared multiple products, and is willing to pay a higher price for a product that fits their needs.»<br /><br />Fewer but more committed customers. But make no mistake, what Maurya posits is no easy task -- it requires discipline, and there is a sanguine optimism that underlies his implicit assumption that you can do it.
0066620996|4.0|Written by famed business consultant, Jim Collins, Good to Great is a book that analyses how a good company can transition into a company that has sustained growth and results.  Unlike Collins' first book, Built to Last, the companies that Collins and his team delved into were not great companies that continued their successes.  Instead, the organizations that were considered were those that were milling along, had a transitional phase or event, and then from there became \\"great\\" companies.  Among those that were analyzed were not only specific organizations but also their competitors to determine what they had done differently to set themselves apart from the industry.<br /> Collins enjoys using metaphors to better relate the point he is attempting to get across to his reader.  The Flywheel is a recurring theme that allows the reader to understand the plight of a company that is attempting to transition from good to great.  The flywheel shows that companies must continuously push forward through the good and bad, the ups and downs and periods of uncertainty and struggles.  Ultimately, great companies, according to Collins' research, show that there are three stages of development which include the company's people, thoughts and actions.<br /> The people that Collins' speaks about revolved around the idea of leadership.  Specifically, that companies who were good and became great had one thing in common, a level five leader.  In contrast to other levels, level five leadership is the culmination of all subsequent stages.  It goes well beyond the productive contributions and achievement of group objectives of a highly capable employee (level one) and team member (level two).  Furthermore, the level five executive expands upon efficiently organizing capital and creating a vision in pursuit of attaining goals and stimulating high performance, the major roles of a competent manger (level three) and effective leader (level four), respectively.  Conversely, level five leadership encompasses all of these characteristics, as well as undeniable humility and resolve that can neither be measured nor simply acquired.  Level five leaders are one of the main driving forces behind good companies becoming great ones.<br /> One aspect of Collins' book that was especially interesting, and one of my favorite parts, was the idea that level five leaders not only focus on the company and its products but much of the success they envision is because of those who surround them.  Level five leaders do not simply have yes-men.  A concept that Collins' stresses for a successful company is the idea of \\"first who, then what.\\"  Another apropos metaphor that Collins' utilizes is that of a school bus.  It is important for an organization to not only get the right people on the bus but also to let the wrong people off the bus.  If everyone is not \\"driving\\" in the same direction, the overall effort will be stymied.  Collins goes one step further in his metaphor and that is it simply isn't enough to have the right people on the bus.  If they are sitting in the wrong seat, they are as useless as those whom they just replaced.  Again, it comes down to a level five leader knowing what has to be done and assigning the right people to do the job at hand.<br /> Another topic that Collins and his team discuss is that of a Hedgehog Concept.  The Hedgehog Concept is the fundamental comparison between a hedgehog and a fox.  While the fox is smooth, cunning, and crafty it is always attempting to outsmart its prey, while never truly mastering a single procedure or method.  This was something the comparison companies did all too often in their pursuit to become great.  However, the companies who actually did take the next step to greatness were more similar to that of the hedgehog, which follows what it knows best being its one simplistic and instinctive instrument to its survival.  The comparison companies constantly tried to pull the wool over the eyes of its customers and, even at times, its employees, while the hedgehog, the great companies, stuck to what made them good companies to being with.  They did not cut corners to get to the Promised Land, something that a level five leader would not allow.<br /> A final aspect that Good to Great touches upon is that of disciplined action.  Technology is an important aspect that many companies use in their quest from mediocre to good and good to great.  Organizations that desire to be the best they can be foster a place of innovation, creativity and ingenuity.  Without supporting those aspects it will be very difficult for a company to transition to great.  In a forever changing world it is necessary to have a constantly changing and developing organization.  A company that rests on its pasts and its laurels is sure to rest where it is and not improve its standing.  Nonetheless, using too much technology and becoming reliant on it to drive a corporation's growth can be equally as stifling.  By following an established strategy, technology and its uses can help further the ideologies and hedgehog concepts of a company instead of being the company.  Technology is simply a support function for greatness, not the source of it.  The source of greatness is having level five leaders push the flywheel through the hedgehog concepts.  Collins stresses that each dimensions relies on the other and greatness cannot be achieved by having only two of the three dynamics.  It is a collaborative effort that everyone must be involved in.  By having one person, either in the wrong seat, on the wrong bus, using fox techniques, or attempting to use an over reliance on technology to take the next step can stymie an organization's growth opportunities and their ability to step up from good to great.<br /> One aspect of Collins' book that I tend to criticize is that of the Hedgehog Concept as, in many institutions of higher education, it is taught that the best way to minimize risk is to diversify and not put allthe eggs in one's proverbial basket.  In the event of a failure, the Hedgehog Concept theory would limit the ability of a corporation to divest itself of its losses and prepare for a slowdown of growth.  One other aspect that was not particularly enjoying about Good to Great was the multitude of story lines that kept on arising as the pages turned.  In an effort to give the reader a better understanding of how upper management functions it became confusing keeping track of which CEO belonged to which company and what the past history of the CEO was.  If one is not familiar with all the names that make up an organization's rankings it can become perplexing trying to figure out who was with what and when and why...in the living room!  A murder mystery of sorts!<br /> In the end, most of the companies that were featured in Good to Great are still in business and doing well for themselves.  The fact that every company did not stand the test of time, especially during a recession unseen in 30 years, does not affect the assumptions in the book.  If a corporation possesses level five leadership and personnel, then there is a favorable chance for success.  Even today, the theories and ideas in the book still apply to nearly any business as technology continues to progress more rapidly now more than ever.  What makes this book outstanding is that Good to Great is not just a practical business application.  Its principles can be practiced by all sorts of different people, regardless of background.  Not only does it reveal how to transition an organization from good to great, but also how to lead a great life.  Every day, we are surrounded by opportunities, and the difficulty is often realizing not what but how, not when but who, and most importantly, not what is the answer, but what is the question.  Only then can one learn how to progress from good to great.

0205313426|3.0|I know this book has its devoted followers who swear it's essential for the act of writing but to me it feels like an ad hoc collection of do's and don'ts which may help you polish up a few details of your writing but won't teach you the basics of how to write clearly.  There may be some useful substance in there somewhere, but I don't find the presentation to be very engaging and I've found other, more recent books that, for me, are better: for instance, \\"Style\\" by Williams and \\"Rhetorical Grammar\\" by Kolln.  These books are influenced by currents in linguisitcs developed since Strunk and White, they center on the idea that \\"norms\\" can vary based on functional needs, and they promote analysis and exercise of judgment rather than sweeping, universal stylistic rules.<br /><br />Strunk and White may have some value as a reference tool in the editing process, it doesn't cost much, and it certainly won't do you any harm, so you might as well buy it; but it doesn't strike me as indispensible.

0439023513|3.0|Spoilers below...<br /><br />I just finished breathlessly finishing the trilogy last night after consecutively reading all 3 books back to back over the span of a few days. All three books were fantastic page turners with rich character development, tremendous imagery, wit, humor, and adventure.<br /><br />However, after finishing the Mockingjay late last night I actually found myself angry at how the story ends and at the author - not for the content, but with the way in which it was treated. After spending all that time really getting to know and develop feelings for the characters, the last 20 pages or so just feels too rushed and artificial.<br /><br />Although there are a few minor nit-picky things throughout, there are two things in particular in the ending that I do not understand and that I feel are not well treated by the author:<br /><br />1) Why did Katniss vote \\"Yes\\" to have a final Hunger Games? Yes, the pain of Prim's death is understandable, but this is completely out of her character based on her past words and deeds and comes as a complete shock. What is the point of this? To show how low she has sunk due to the effects of battle and emptiness? To put her into sharper contrast with Peeta's humanity who instantly rejects the idea? To simply make the upcoming death of Coin more unexpected?  Worst of all, there is no mention that after the vote and Coin's death that this final Games is not going to happen. Afterall, the victors did vote for it, right? I don't get it. Maybe someone can help me out here.<br /><br />2) This is the biggest one for me: I do not understand why Katniss ends up with Peeta. The worst part is that after all the love turmoil throughout the whole trilogy, she isn't even the one who makes the final decision. The boys do. Peeta basically shows up (apparantly as soon as the Dr. allowed him) but Gale simply doesn't come back, accepting a \\"fancy job\\" in District 2? Uhhh... what? I get the author's attempt to say that Katniss needed thewarmth and eternal optimism of Peeta to find peace, but I cannot forgive the author for simply allowing Gale to never come back or say anything. This is completely out of his character. Worst of all you get the sense that in her heart Katniss deep down still wants Gale although she is willing to accept Peeta. Even after Peeta had shown up. When she went out to the woods and sat on their meeting rock, if Gale had shown up (as she kept wishing he would) saying, \\"Hey, Catnip\\" or something, I'm left feeling she would have run off with him in a heartbeat. Perhaps in Gale's final conversation with Katniss where she connects him to Prim's death, he feels that she will truly never accept him now and he leaves to save himself the anguish of trying to love this now broken, cold woman, finally deciding he must move on. But at the time of the conversation Katniss herself already knows it wasn't his fault and wants to forgive him, she just can't right then. We'll just never know. I'm left feeling that Katniss has \\"settled\\" with Peeta simply because Gale didn't bother coming back. And for this I am left feeling unhappy for all three characters. Maybe this was the point, but I don't know why.<br /><br />I'm fine with unhappy endings, but to not tell why Gale chooses to not come back or fully explain his reasons is almost unforgiveable as their unresolved love is one of the most important themes in the series. And then to include an epilogue that makes it clear that Gale never comes back even denies me the freedom to envision other eventual final endings in my own head. It is easy to come up with \\"shock\\" endings, but the way this one ends just feels ... sloppy. And after spending all that time connecting with the characters, I feel a little cheated and empty.

No matter what you may think of him personally, there is an effortless charm that he brings to almost all of his films. Lately, though, it's been a bit of a slog. Abrams action; but like a snowball rolling down a hill, once it starts, it just gets bigger and bigger and then you find yourself at the bottom of that hill and you await the bliss of the impact. This may work for some readers of fantasy fiction.  They like the complexity--and believe me there is enough complexity to satisfy most readers of fiction, and the nuanced characters, no black and white or true good and evil here.  But I need some satisfaction.  If I wanted misery and death all the time I would read about world politics, The Sudan, Afghanistan, Iraq.  I have read about 1500 pages into the series, and nothing nice has happened since the first few chapters. The stories are intricate, the writing is compelling, the characters are enjoyably wicked and enjoyably worth rooting for.  But the books are a drag.

B00756GKBA|5.0|As a thirty-three year old man, I've been a fan of Madonna since I was five or six years old, back then much to my mother's chagrin.  So I grew up watching this woman reinvent herself and pop music over and over and over again.  As her career advanced and her album count grew, Madonna took over a great majority of the song-writing duties (1989's Like a Prayer), began managing her own career with Maverick Records (under the Warner Bros. umbrella), and shocked the world many times over with her blatant sexual and religious imagery.<br /><br />Now, I am not one of those fans who blindly adores everything my favorite artists do.  In fact I think a true fan will admit when they don't necessarily care for something one of their favorite artists do.  It shows a willingness to actually peel back the layers and examine the piece from different perspectives.  2008's Hard Candy as a whole truly disappointed me.  The disc definitely boasted some great tunes.  The lead single \\"4 Minutes,\\" featuring Justin Timberlake and Timbaland was catchy and fun, and I enjoyed the more adult-contemporary ballad-like third single \\"Miles Away,\\" which seemed to indicate trouble in paradise for Madge and Guy Ritchie.  I also really enjoyed \\"Heartbeat\\" and \\"Devil Wouldn't Recognize You.\\"  Beyond those four tracks, everything else was just okay or mediocre in my opinion.  Pharrell Williams' influence as a co-writer is very evident and as much I enjoy Pharrell as an artist, I do not feel the urban pop vibe fit Madonna very well, though I absolutely commended her for even bothering to go there.<br /><br />When I heard M.D.N.A.'s lead single, \\"Give Me All Your Luvin',\\" I was equally disappointed and very, very vocal about my disappointment.  Even though Hard Candy did hit #1 on the album charts, it sold 500,000 copies.  This was rather paltry considering her previous effort, Confessions on a Dance Floor (released at the tail end of 2005), sold 350,000 copies in its first week.  To date, Confessions...has sold over 3 million copies.  I had been hoping Madonna would return to her Confessions formula.  After all, Madonna is the Queen of Pop; she's pretty much been everywhere and she's done everything.  There aren't really many more envelopes Madonna could realistically push and keep her artistic integrity intact.  The euro-synth pop of Confessions suits Madonna much more than the urban pop of Hard Candy.<br /><br />M.D.N.A.'s second single, \\"Girl Gone Wild,\\" gave me hope.  The confection of hard driving beats and synth-pop, along with catchy lyrics and a killer (and HOT!!) music video gave me hope I would enjoy the new disc.  Much to my surprise M.D.N.A is definitely more like Confessions than Hard Candy, while still managing to break new ground.  In fact, I'd say M.D.N.A. combines the euro-synth pop of Confessions with dramatic infusions of the guitar/electronica of Ray of Light.  Speaking of Ray of Light, producer William Orbit is back and contributes his genius to this record.  Benny Benassi and Martin Solveig round out the list of major producers, although Alle Benassi, Demolition Crew, Free School, Indiigo, and Michael Malih contribute to production.<br /><br />M.D.N.A. is full of amazing material. Sedaris has a unique perspective and style.  You like them or you don't.  Me: I like them.  I don't turn to Sedaris for enlightenment, challenge, depth; I turn to him for entertainment and a bit of light voyeuristic escapism.  The one thing I can't help but wonder, though, is whether all the stories he tells are true.  How many genuinely quirky experiences can one man have -- let alone recall in such detail?  I'd certainly feel cheated to learn they're not true, not simply that they stretch the truth within the bounds of creative license.  But I can't imagine that any dogged fact checkers are out there seeking to verify the details of Sedaris' kicked smoking habit or his parents' art collection.  So, I'll have to take a lot on faith.  Easy enough: it's fun to read Sedaris.  As long as he continues cranking out his life vignettes, I'll continue reading them.

0439023521|5.0|All Katniss Everdeen has ever known is the poverty and hunger of life in the Seam, the poorest part of District 12 in the country of Panem. Panem rose from the ashes of what was once North America, now a shining Capitol that wields an iron control over its twelve districts, with an empty thirteenth left wasted as a warning to any who dream of defying the Capitol's rule. The hardscrabble existence of life in one of the poorest districts robbed Katniss of her beloved father, forcing her into the role of family breadwinner through illegal hunting and trade. When Reaping Day arrives -- the annual event when one boy and one girl are randomly chosen to fight in the Hunger Games -- Katniss's world is rocked when her beloved sister Prim is selected as tribute. With no thought but saving her sister, Katniss volunteers to take her place, catapulting her into the national spotlight. The Games are a brutal reminder of the Capitol's iron control, a world Katniss is determined to survive. In a world that seeks to strip individuals of their humanity and individuality, a world that demands unquestioning obedience and conformity, can a mere girl with an unquenchable fire in her survive? For Katniss is determined not to go quietly into that dark night that the Games demands...<br /><br />The Hunger Games is a wholly absorbing novel of one girl's fight to survive against a system that has reduced her existence, her hope of a future, to a mere \\"entertainment.\\" Through Collins's relentless, tense, urgent prose we're thrust deep into Katniss's point-of-view. She's an interesting first person narrator -- because of her youth it is perhaps tempting to question the reliability of her viewpoint. But in the end it is the fact that she is such a mass of contradictions -- mature beyond her years as the family provider, jaded, a hardened survivalist, yet uncertain of her own self-worth and unaware of the effect she has on others -- that in the end is what allows one to fully appreciate the horrific implications of the Hunger Games. For Katniss hails from a future where violent death is the biggest entertainment and the greatest weapon the government possesses, where a tribute is reduced to a pawn, their innocence subjugated to society's bloodlust. And the greatest risk Katniss can take is opening her heart to the realization that she is worthy of a love beyond comprehension -- and the greatest rebellion is daring to dictate one's last breath. Collins's first venture into the world of Panem and its districts is a completely absorbing read, a wild, pulse-pounding ride that grabbed me by the throat and didn't release me until the final page. And even then, the power of this story remained.<br /><br />Collins's invention of the Hunger Games is a searing indictment of our self-gratifying, 24-hour news cycle, reality entertainment obsessed culture, wrapped in a relentlessly thrilling package. There is no time wasted diving deeply into the bleak world of District 12 -- this is a new reality that the narrative drives us to accept in the same manner Katniss and her peers matter-of-factly accept the requirement of tribute and teenage death matches as a simple fact of life. The shock is jarring, Collins daring readers to examine our own culture and its -- dare we think it -- similarities to Panem in the objectification of human life through our entertainment, both reality and scripted, that sterilizes the cost of war, reducing human pain to an arena event.<br /><br />For all the horror of the harsh reality Collins presents, it's shot through with rays of hope, unexpected moments of light that shine all the brighter for the bleak, repressive regime of Panem with its callous disregard for basic humanity. Peeta, Katniss's fellow tribute from District 12, is a gorgeously-rendered example of humility, self-sacrifice, and love in a world gone mad -- a love so pure, so without reservation, that Katniss simply cannot comprehend it when they first meet. Of course, by focusingon Katniss's coming-of-age journey -- and by association Peeta's as well -- Collins never really fleshes out the identities of their fellow tributes. By making Katniss and Peeta the most sympathetic characters, the ones who capture readers' hearts, it's tempting to forget the cost of their victory -- until we see it etched in the emotional wounds of their hollow victory.<br /><br />The Hunger Games is a challenging, thought-provoking thrill ride. A gripping, well-plotted novel that's nearly flawless in its execution, in its ability to draw you into its world, make you emotionally invested in the leads, and then make you question how you view this story, how many steps our own culture is from the world of the Games, because after all, haven't we been there before with Rome and her coliseum? Curtis witnesses coming storms that only he seems to see. He begins exhibiting bizarre behavior that threatens his relationship with his wife, jeopardizes his job, and undermines his credibility in the community. Writer-director Jeff Nichols masterfully weaves a complex tale of one man's apparent mental collapse and its consequences on those around him--but strong undercurrents of uncertainty permeate throughout this film. Is Curtis really losing his mind? Are his visions and dreams really omens of what is to come? Never before have I rooted so hard for a character's sanity. The tension mounts to an almost unbearable climax--only to yield to a truly remarkable final scene. See this movie--it is a powerful and majestic work of cinematic art.

If this is giving rise to a new genre (their calling it \\"mommy porn\\")to be read quickly and not provoke thought, then it suceeds. Unfortuantely it also seems to work as a movie pitch. Given that movies generally provide less depth than the books they are based upon, I do not see how this could be further diluted.

B004IFYMYI|5.0|Travis Bickle (Robert DeNiro) is a former Marine and a veteran of the Vietnam war. He frequently has trouble sleeping and is plagued by headaches. The pills he takes don't seem to help at all. Because he's likely to be awake all night anyway, Bickle takes a job driving a cab. He prefers the night shift.<br /><br />Scorsese's version of New York is gritty and realistic. Most of the film is shot at night and we see just how crazy some of the people are. It's a crime-ridden world populated by junkies and prostitutes. Bickle hates how much scum exists in the city, and he's referring to the people more than the dirty streets.<br /><br />Bickle is looking for something to brighten up his seedy existence and a woman catches his eye. Betsy (Cybill Shepherd) works in a local office for a senator who is hoping to become the next president. After pestering her for a while, she eventually agrees to go on a date with him. He talks to her in a very straightforward way. It's not clear whether he lacks intelligence, or if he doesn't see any point in hiding the truth about himself. His approach works and leads to one of the funniest scenes in the film when he takes her to see a movie.<br /><br />Other things attract Bickle's attention. He's particularly drawn to Iris (Jodie Foster), who is a 12-year-old prostitute. As his frustration mounts at the disgusting state of the city, he begins to devise a plan to clean up one small part. An incident on the street gives him the idea of saving Iris from her situation and giving her the opportunity to escape from her pimp (Harvey Keitel).<br /><br />It's interesting watching Bickle trying to escape his miserable existence. He figures out how to buy and use weapons and starts working out to strengthen his body. All the while, there's mounting tension, and we sense that Bickle is close to losing touch with reality completely.<br /><br />The supporting cast is strong, with Shepherd, Foster and Keitel all playing their parts well. You'll also see Peter Boyle and Albert Brooks. One of the most surprising scenes involves a cameo from Scorsese. His character might even be the catalyst for the change which eventually occurs in Bickle.<br /><br />I have to mention Scorsese's decision to shoot most of the scenes at night. It was an inspired choice and adds so much atmosphere to the film. Bernard Herrmann's saxophone-heavy score is also an important part of the mix and helps helps transport us into Bickle's troubled world.<br /><br />De Niro has produced some incredible performances over the past 40 years and Travis Bickle is probably my favorite De Niro character. There's so much going on beneath the surface and De Niro portrays much of it with his body language.<br /><br />The meaning of the conclusion is open to debate, but if you take it purely at face value, it's quite uplifting.<br /><br />The Blu-ray presentation adds a lot to the experience and is a must-own for fans of the film.Peter Boyle and Albert Brooks. Right now I believe I am near the end of the 3rd novel, in the 48,863 range.  The dots at the bottom of my e-reader are almost 3/4 filled.  Several times now I've wished I just purchased the hardbound books.  This is my first experience with buying an e-bundle series and I would be hesitant to purchase another for fear they are all formatted like this.
0307719219|2.0|The most startling revelation provided by this book is the total disconnect between, on the one hand, the hype (see amazon page) and the credentials of the authors and, on the other, the actual delivered contents.  The first author has a chair at MIT and a John Bates Clark medal, the second has a chair at Harvard. But surely, please, not for this sort of thing.  They have one idea, which, in so far as it has any substance, is that 'extractive institutions are bad'.  They claim to find extractive institutions where bad things are, and not where good things are.  They don't actually provide a useful definition of what an extractive institution is, though they have a long list of places where things did not go well, which are supposed to illustrate 'extractive' situations, and places where things did go well as not.  This vagueness is sensible, because they apply the label to so many different things that there is no possibility that I could see of a useful operational definition.<br /><br />Of the many mangy hedgehogs I have encountered in 'one big idea' world history, this is surely the mangiest.<br /><br />They manage, for instance, to be confident that they can diagnose 'extractive' institutions in Mayan civilization and various neolithic arrangements in the middle east.  Their claims about the fine structure of political institutions in 2000BC in what was to become Syria, or where-ever it was, are on the basis of distributions of flint arrow heads and the like.  Never do they discuss (or even appear to stop to wonder) what, exactly, a non-extractive institution would look like in situations like this, or how you would distinguish it on the basis of the surviving archeological evidence.<br /><br />One thing I was interested to read to was their prognosis for China, but this boils down to very little more than 'China has bad institutions, which are bad because in the long term they won't allow the sort of creative destruction that an economy cannot avoid if it is going to continue to grow to rich nation levels of prosperity'.  Really?<br /><br />If you have read people like Mancur Olson, and your ideas about political economy and political sociology are even a little bit more sophisticated than what you can find in nerdy libertarian science fiction (or, come to think of it, than you can learn from full professors at MIT or Harvard), then there is no insight here worth stopping for that I could identify.  Not that I actually stopped for all four hundred-odd pages; after a while, I simply got tired ploughing through endless pages of low-content penny-a-word prose and started skimming more and more rapidly (even on its own terms, this is at most a hundred page essay, not a four hundred page book).<br /><br />For this level of insight I paid 14EUR?  Oswald Spengler is at least fun to read.<br /><br />P.S. [addendum that occurred to me after a recent evening run] This is not a history book.  The authors are not historians, and they do not think like historians (they are an economist and a political scientist); they have no interest in the past except to cherry pick examples to relate to the present.  This is essentially a disposable policy wonk essay blown up to a book, and all the hype is in policy wonk circles, not historian circles.  Its purpose is to reassure a specific class of policy wonks who are desperate to be reassured by what they see as figures of authority (MIT economist, Harvard political scientist), that, in spite of current pessimism, yes, liberal democratic capitalism is the best way to arrange a society, and it will prevail (that there is no Untergang des Abendlandes, if you like).  This may be true (I even think so - the first bit, at least), but this book makes no original or substantial case, it's just a (shallow, whiggish) sermon to the converted.  Interestingly, the authors even implicitly acknowledge that what they have written is a disposable policy wonk essay, since they chose to issue it in a 'business book' imprint, and not with Cambridge or Princeton University press, which is where they usually publish (or maybe CUP and PUP simply declined the offer).

B004XTWRYQ|4.0|Jackdaws is an exciting espionage thriller set in World War II with a bit of a twist: it's about a team of British spies operating behind enemy lines, who are all women (including a couple of lesbians)...well, and one German transvestite.<br /><br />The heroines are very interesting (particularly the transvestite), but Follett's greatest achievement in this novel is perhaps his villain, a German interrogator in charge of rooting out and breaking up the French resistance.  He is not averse to using extreme brutal torture to extract information if necessary (and there is some very graphic violence in the novel, but with the Nazis as a subject that's pretty much inevitable), but he is extremely intelligent and would rather use trickery or emotional manipulation when those methods would serve the purpose.  In fact, he finds the methods of his fellow Nazis rather distasteful.<br /><br />At the same time, Follett portrays the lead heroine, Flick, as somewhat cold and steely, willing to kill the enemy in cold blood to protect her mission.  Many writers today would use this juxtaposition to insinuate that the Nazis weren't such monsters as they are often made out to be and that the Allies were just as brutal and vicious.  But in Follett's hands, the Nazi interrogator's supposed \\"scruples\\" are clearly rank hypocrisy which only serves to highlight the atrocious nature of the ends he pursues and the means he employs, while Flick's actions are underlain by a righteous determination not to suffer the guilt which rightly belongs to the aggressors against whom she fights.  Very well done.<br /><br />Kate Reading's narration of this audio edition is excellent, bringing out the emotional depths of Follett's story.  I would definitely recommend it.

But instead of making characters flawed and human and sympathetic, everyone ends up feeling arbitrary and devoid of motivation. I had to force myself to finish the book just so i could say that I completed it.  If you pick up this book, which if you've read the previous two you will, be prepared to read it just to finish it. B00756GKBA|4.0|Madonna is a fascinating and complex woman, full of contradictions and virtues and vices. On her new record, her first in four years and her first since liberating herself from two powerful (and possibly oppressive) forces (Warner Brothers and Guy Ritchie), she seems less interested than ever in reconciling all of them. \\"MDNA\\" is a sonic portrait of a middle-aged global pop superstar embracing everything that she is.<br /><br />1. First and foremost, Madonna likes to get her boogie on. She's spent her career finding new and exciting ways to keep the world dancing, and MDNA's tracks are filled with inventive, head-spinning grooves.<br /><br />2. Madonna likes to experiment, always pushing the boundaries of how far pop music can go. The album's second track, \\"Gang Bang\\", is a dark revenge fantasy that sounds like a Quentin Tarantino film on ecstasy. The album closes with \\"Falling Free,\\" a beautiful, beat-less track that sounds like a showtune from another planet. Those are probably the most out-there songs, but her innovative spirit shows through on almost every tune.<br /><br />3. Madonna has a complicated relationship with religion. She's been talking about Kaballah for years, but \\"Girl Gone Wild,\\" \\"I'm a Sinner,\\" and \\"I Don't Give A ...\\" all name drop Catholicism and Christianity.<br /><br />4. Madonna is a romantic. Whether gushing about a new lover (\\"I'm Addicted\\"; \\"Superstar\\") or lamenting love gone bad (\\"Love Spent\\"), romantic love is never far from her mind. When she's not making dance tracks, she also has a long history of making poignant ballads like \\"Masterpiece\\", and it's often those times where her singing voice really shines.<br /><br />5. Madonna is resilient. Most of her contemporaries are either dead or have faded into obscurity. As a 53-year old mother of four, she's still selling out massive world tours, still rocking a body that most 20-year-olds would kill for, and still making albums that make some other of-the-moment pop stars look like amateurs.<br /><br />As Nicki Minaj says, \\"there's only one Queen, and that's Madonna.\\" Long live the Queen..<br /><br />As Nicki Minaj says, \\"there's only one Queen, and that's Madonna.\\" Long live the Queen.
B00756GKBA|5.0|MDNA opens with the second worldwide single release, Girl Gone Wild. One of three tracks produced by the Benassi team, \\"Girl\\" starts the record with an upbeat Italian club-pop track that keeps the beats heavy while the lyrics are a bit too light for my liking.<br />Gang Bang and I'm Addicted move away from standard pop to an almost industrial, EBM sound. The bass is thick and heavy, the mood is dark, and Madonna is a woman scorned in a Tarantino flick in Gang Bang.<br />I'm Addicted also has elements of early 80's sounds from the likes of \\"Soft Cell,\\" mixed with early 90's techno elements similar to \\"Utah Saints.\\" In the middle breakdown, Madonna, gives a nod to her own track \\"Future Lovers\\" before the, Mika penned, track goes into hi-speed and shoots us into space while the pop icon chants \\"MDNA\\" over and over again.<br /><br />Arguably the biggest pop hit on the album, Turn Up the Radio was originally intended for Solveig's 2011 album, \\"Smash\\" and originally sung by \\"Sunday Girl.\\" Madonna, with her added touches, turns his original track into a fantastic sunny delight reminiscent of that mid 90's euro sound mixed in with current french house and the result is a feel good, summer club anthem for 2012.<br />Give Me All Your Luvin is not representative of anything we've heard so far on the record and may have been the worst song to pick to introduce MDNA. On the album itself, \\"Give Me\\" fits like a glove and segues perfectly from \\"Turn Up the Radio,\\" to maintain the high energy of the first part of the album, to lead into the less pop/dance next section.<br /><br />Some Girls marks a clear shift from the dancier euro metallic tech-house of the first section to a more, raw, electro, NY early 80's vibe with Blondie style vocal verses and for the chorus.  To be honest, I was expecting much more from a Klaas and Madonna collaboration.<br />Superstar is a simple pop track, in the vein of old pop style hits like \\"Cherish\\" and \\"True Blue.\\" The track receives background vocal assistance from none other than M's daughter, Lourdes but doesn't seem to \\"fit\\" on the album.<br />I Don't Give A takes us back to Madonna rapping, American Life style, on a beat that sounds somewhat amateurish and unrefined. The track would have been mostly filler except for the slightly more substantial lyrics while Nicki Minaj comes in and does a great job taking the track where it needed to go. Before the track ends with a full minute dramatic instrumental, Nicki Minaj claims, \\"There's only one Queen, and that's Madonna...\\"<br /><br />The next two William Orbit tracks are album highlights for me.  I'm a Sinner is the song that returns Madonna and Orbit back to \\"Ray of Light,\\" \\"Beautiful Stranger\\" territory. The track is fun, psychedelic and Madonna sounds pristine.  Love Spent is a wild ride that starts the track with a banjo that kicks into a slick beat and some of Madonna's best vocals. During the first half of this dancey electro auto-biographical piece, Madonna sings in a certain style on the chorus, and then two minutes into the track the banjo comes in again but this time when the song starts, its an entirely new song, new chorus and new energy. This dream-like second half of Love-Spent, with a pounding back beat, is sadder and lonelier. This track is essentially two different songs and two different emotions and stands as a major album highlight.<br />Closing out the standard edition of the album are two more William Orbit ballads. Masterpiece took home the Golden Globe Award for \\"Best Song\\" from a movie (W.E.) and takes us back to ballad sounds that came from \\"Something to Remember\\" and \\"Ray of Light.\\"<br />Falling Free is a stunning departure in sound from the rest of the album and the music sounds like something that would have been produced by the likes of Kate Bush (Wuthering Heights) or Tori Amos. Also similar in style to Madonna's own tracks, \\"Frozen,\\" \\"Mer Girl,\\" and \\"Has To Be,\\" Falling Free contains some of Madonna's most impressive vocals.<br/><br />The Deluxe version of the album contains five more tracks (one being a remix). Beautiful Killer, an electro dance tune in the vein of \\"Let It Will Be\\" from her Confessions album along with 80's dance beats, is sleek and sexy and deserved a place on the standard edition of the album as does the next track.<br />I \\"effed\\" Up is a gorgeous pop ballad who's contemplative yet simple lyrics bring chills to my body and make me feel sympathetic to Madonna and wonder what she went through in her marriage. As I listen to this, I wonder could she be singing this just to Guy, or could it be to her brother Christopher, or maybe her legions of gay fans who feel that she has long forgotten them as she now makes documentaries about her domestic life and prefers to discuss her faith over her politics. On this album, Madonna gives a wink to the gays and reminds them who it was that was pushing expression, love and tolerance in the 80's and 90's.<br />The album goes into a psychedelic fluffy filler ala Cher's \\"Beat Goes On,\\" titled, \\"Bday Song\\" with another guest feature from M.I.A. and finally closes with \\"Best Friend,\\" a forgettable jittery R&B electro slow-jam.<br /><br />Madonna's albums of the last decade generally maintained a certain style of sound or theme throughout the entire record typically because she worked only with one or two producers for each album. This album see's her with no less than four different producers and because of this there's a disjointed feeling but on the bright side really gives us the opportunity to let MDNA be our soundtrack whether we are in the mood to dance or in the mood to cry. MDNA takes us on several different journeys and moods, taking the best of all her albums of the last decade and spitting out a shiny, black, euphoric, disco pill.
