B00756GKBA|4.0|Before I get into the actual review, let me preface it by saying that I am a huge Madonna fan and have been for most of my 30 years. Her music and messages have inspired me through good times and bad, personally and professionally; I have over 200 CD's from around the world and a Madonna tattoo on my leg, so trust me, I know what I'm talking about, lol. But in a weird way I'm also sort of the \\"anti-fan,\\" because nothing annoys me more than those fans who are so busy falling over themselves in giddy fits of \\"OMG my Queen is so perfect in every way!\\" that they never really take the time to stop and THINK if what she does is actually good or not, and why. The yes-men who eat up everything Madonna does solely out of habit give the real fans like me, who actually use their brain to evaluate things objectively - which of course is what Madonna is all about - a bad name. (Don't get me wrong, I used to be the same way...when I was 12. The throngs of middle-aged gay men lining up to buy 6 bottles of her new ladies' perfume simply because it has her name on the bottle, however, are a complete embarrassment to my kind.) Point is, for every person who hates Madonna blindly, there are another two or three who love her blindly, and that's just as bad. Know that my opinion, while sure to ruffle some feathers, will be as honest, thoughtful, and most importantly, informed, as I can possibly make it.<br /><br />So with that said, I feel no shame whatsoever in admitting I absolutely loathed her last studio album, 2008's Hard Candy, and was half afraid she'd never recover from what seemed like the kiss of death to her otherwise artfully illustrious career. For the first time ever it seemed as if she were the follower and not the leader, hopping on the disgusting \\"let's feature an interchangeable rapper in every song so we can get airplay on American radio\\" bandwagon, which still totally backfired since no one wants to acknowledge that anyone over 25 exists in pop culture anyway, much less a white woman in her 50's trying to be \\"street.\\" Age aside, it was an obvious act of desperation. \\"Oh, she's just having fun and not trying to make a statement this time,\\" my fellow fans would say. Well, that's fine, but if she really was having fun, I'll eat my foot, because her voice was painfully flat and lifeless, and she sounded terribly bored as if she herself subconsciously (or perhaps consciously?) knew she was selling out. Not that I necessarily expect her to reinvent the wheel with each new record, either, but if she as the artist isn't having fun, I as the consumer am not having fun. (Even the videos from this era are totally phoned in; I was surprised she knew how to be that lazy. Thankfully, she seemed less awkward with the new material during the accompanying Sticky and Sweet Tour performances, but only just.) To sum it up, aside from two or three brief moments, Hard Candy was basically bad music, done badly, and we shall never speak of it again.<br /><br />Then she sort of went undercover, released a so-so greatest hits package, directed a so-so movie, performed a fabulous Superbowl halftime show that I'm sure Elton John just loved the heck out of, and now here she is again, four years later, with MDNA, the new album the world was waiting for with baited breath. (I'm told in addition to being a play on her own name, the title is also a cheeky allusion to the drug ecstasy. As someone who has never touched even a single cigarette in his entire life, the reference was lost on me.) Now, to be perfectly blunt, Give Me All Your Luvin', the first single that leaked several months prior, initially suggested to me that she finally gave up caring about music altogether. Juvenile lyrics, wooden vocals, and obnoxious \\"been there, done that\\" guest spots by Nicki Minaj and M.I.A. does not a hit make. Well, these days I suppose it does, actually, although I can't claim to know anybody firsthand who enjoys having their pop songs ruined by ghetto rap interludes. But `tis the norm now, so I digress. I finally had to admit it was growing on me when I found myself serenading the dogs with cheerleader chants, so it did ultimately prove to be a little catchy, and there's at least something to be said for that aspect of it. Plus the video was kinda cute and she looked freaking amazing in it. Another song I heard early on was the Golden Globe winning ballad Masterpiece, which was also featured in her film W.E., and it was love at first listen. While not quite up there with the likes of Take a Bow, Live to Tell, or even This Used to Be My Playground, it's a magnificent song with a beautiful arrangement and heartfelt lyrics sung so poetically that I can easily say it's her best song, period, in at least the last 6 or 7 years. So far it seemed like a mixed bag, but by this point I considered myself cautiously optimistic about her record as a whole and whatever chameleonic incarnation she was about to unleash upon the world. (I'm not even going to address the fact this is Madonna's first CD in a post-Gaga universe. They are both great - and very different -  artists in their own right, and the idiots who perpetuate the idea that you can't enjoy one just because you do like the other need serious professional help. It's not a competition and there's no reason whatsoever to measure them against eachother.)<br /><br />So let's cut to the chase - is MDNA any good? I'm glad to report that, for the most part, YES, yes it is! It doesn't exactly scream instant classic, mind you, but as a follow up to the cringe-worthy Hard Candy, it definitely redeems Madonna as a credible pop artist. My initial impression of a mixed bag wasn't too far off, and like any record, there are high points and low points, a few of which I'll touch on briefly. The album's opener and second single, Girl Gone Wild, is a frantic, fast-paced powerhouse of a song that almost ventures into rave territory, so have your glow sticks close at hand and get ready to sweat. Turn Up the Radio is another carefree, commercially safe, pulsating club thumper that I'd guess will eventually be a single as well. Gang Bang is a somewhat demented spoken-word track in which we find a scorned Madonna fantasizing about shooting her lover in the head, and one which would not be entirely out of place on the Erotica album. Love it! Falling Free is the Mer Girl of the album, with no real chorus or loop to speak of, just Madonna's lush vocals over a minimal, meandering musical swell. It's one of the few songs in recent memory that proves Madonna really CAN sing when she feels like it. Love Spent didn't really hold my attention at first, but repeat listenings reveal a lot of interesting things happening musically that give it a unique \\"texture,\\" if that makes sense. It still isn't the strongest hook you'll ever hear, but it's definitely shaping up to be a favorite. Despite the crass title, I F'd Up is a lovely semi-ballad which has Madonna admitting she isn't the easiest person to live with. A nice complement to the rest of the album's abrasive \\"no regrets\\" theme that fits oddly well. I think Superstar was supposed to be Madonna showing her cutesy, lovey-dovey side a la Cherish, even featuring her daughter Lourdes on background vocals, but unfortunately the lyrics are so corny, if not downright peculiar, it's hard to take the underlying sentiment seriously. Not horrible per se, but Little Star this is not. I'm Addicted has Madonna doing her best Kylie Minogue impression, and the results are sketchy. I love some good techno, but to my ears this fan favorite comes off as a sort of remix gone wrong, amounting to basically one big pile of random noise. (And this is from someone who LOVED Impressive Instant!) Some Girls is another track that falls victim to overproduction; I'm sure \\"on paper\\" it was meant to be a statement about Madonna's place in an industry that's become overrun by 20-somethings, but her voice is so drowned out you can't hear a word and the effect is lost. Professional party rockers LMFAO, who I admit can be sorta fun in their own right, further slaughtered Give Me all Your Luvin' with their bonus remix by utilizing but a small snippet of Madonna's vocals and adding even more tired rap sections. Sigh. At least by the end of it you'll probably appreciate the tepid original version a little bit more. Lastly, I Don't Give A with Nicki Minaj (again) is so atrocious on every level I congratulate the few of you who make it past the first 20 seconds.<br /><br />As you can see, MDNA is a varied album that offers up a little something for everyone. And though it may not have broken any new ground, I don't think it necessarily strived to either. That's fine, because I believe even Madonna's most ardent supporters would admit we're not likely to ever get another Like a Prayer or Ray of Light out of her. However, the record succeeds in its intentions where Hard Candy miserably failed - this is about as youthful, refreshed, and playful as I've seen her in quite a while, and for the first time in recent memory I'm willing to believe that just maybe she is genuinely having a little bit of fun. Combined with the moments of candid darkness, intensity, and general diva-ness, this is, in theory, the perfect Madonna record. I think the thing that keeps it from being truly great is the fact that she chose to work with SO many producers who each want a piece of the pie, that Madonna herself at times comes in as an afterthought or is forced to play second fiddle to someone else's \\"hot beats.\\" It's not the worst case of too many cooks in the kitchen I've seen her mixed up in, but something about the flow and consistency of the album strikes me as off, though many elements work surprisingly well individually. Another sour point is that her lyrics these days tend to be derivative of her own earlier work, which would be fine if it seemed she were intentionally paying homage to herself in a clever wink wink, nudge nudge sort of way. Rather, it more often comes off as though she has simply run out of ideas and may not even realize she's recycling entire phrases. On the bright side, we aren't getting 12 songs built around some form of the word \\"hesitation\\" this time either, lol, so it's progress. Taken together I figure it all balances out to a 3.5 star rating. In short, what's good is REALLY good, but nearly half the disc is comprised of filler that doesn't leave much of an impression one way or the other. I'm generously rounding up to 4 stars because in spite of that criticism, only a small portion is what I'd dare to label as actually being \\"bad.\\"<br /><br />In the end MDNA is very much a reflection of its time; overall she seems perfectly content in embracing the current trends in pop music and just putting her own spin on them, rather than exhausting the effort to be innovative and stay ahead of the curve. Hence this CD probably won't be one of the more celebrated/remembered entries in her catalogue down the road. I understand that approach, since I imagine most \\"classic\\" artists are feeling the pressure to stay relevant these days more than ever, but it does make you wonder how far the uber-ballsy Madonna of decades past would make it as a new act in 2012. Anyhow...Where she goes from here I'm not sure. But as it stands now, despite a rocky patch here and there, she still has all my L-U-V.p the Radio is another carefree, commercially safe, pulsating club thumper that I'd guess will eventually be a single as well. Gang Bang is a somewhat demented spoken-word track in which we find a scorned Madonna fantasizing about shooting her lover in the head, and one which would not be entirely out of place on the Erotica album. Love it! Falling Free is the Mer Girl of the album, with no real chorus or loop to speak of, just Madonna's lush vocals over a minimal, meandering musical swell. B003EYVXV4|5.0|I've never read the books and didn't know what to expect from the Hunger Games except that it was a combination of The Lottery crossed with The Running Man for preteen girls; or, if you know your Japanese literature, Battle Royale.  I was pleasantly surprised to discover it is so much more than that.<br /><br />The future of The Hunger Games is basically a Roman Empire updated to modern times.  In that respect it reminded of the role-playing game X-Crawl, which did much the same thing with a fantasy twist.  There are twelve districts that send \\"tributes\\" to the Hunger Games, which are basically televised gladiatorial combats in which children slaughter each other. Every year a raffle known as a reaping is held to choose a male and female between the ages of 12 and 18.  There can be only one winner. Being televised, The Hunger Games is influenced by the upper class citizens of the dominating Capitol district; contestants are interviewed before the game and during the event can win sponsorships, which in turn earns in-game advantages like healing ointments.<br /><br />Katniss Everdeen (Jennifer Lawrence) volunteers to join the Hunger Games when her little sister Primrose (Willow Shields) is selected by lottery.  Her male companion is Peeta Mellark (Josh Hutcherson) and his complicated past with Katniss will affect their relationship and the game. The game itself takes place on what can only be described as a holodeck like arena that can be manipulated by technicians directed by Gamemaker Seneca Crane (Wes Bentley).<br /><br />Each team has its own entourage, including public relations (Elizabeth Banks as the grating Effie Trinket), a trainer (the drunk Haymitch Abernathy played by a probably drunk Woody Harrelson), and a personal stylist (Cinna, played by Lenny Kravitz channeling Prince).  These five characters manage to gel nicely on screen: Lawrence plays Katniss as aloof and frequently terrified, but with enough steely resolve that you believe she can kill; Hutcherson looks even more terrified than Lawrence and in a twist plays the \\"damsel in distress\\" to Katniss' vigorous presence; Banks is a cartoonish reminder of what Capitol citizens are like (clowns, basically); Harrelson channels frustrated class rage that keeps him in his spirits until he realizes Katniss has a chance at winning; and finally Kravitz does an excellent job of being the only sane person who seems to care about Katniss as a human being.  Director Gary Ross knew what he was doing when he cast this little dysfunctional family.<br /><br />If this all sounds like grim stuff on screen, it is. The Hunger Games is particularly gut-wrenching for parents.  The children can be as young as 12, so there are little ones who are far out of their depth. To the film's credit The Hunger Games never flinches from its basic premise of kids murdering each other.  Although they are more likely to die of deprivation and disease, the Gamemakers force them into conflict with forest fires and even monsters.  And it's all televised around the clock.<br /><br />Lurking in the background is the political unrest that The Hunger Games elicit, particularly over the slaughter of 23 children each year.  While some districts train their children for combat and thus volunteer, the other districts enter unwillingly, which has consequences for the political stranglehold the Capitol lords over them all.  The state-mandated executions have all the pomp and circumstance of a Roman gladiatorial bout.<br /><br />The Hunger Games is of particular interest to gamers.  Video gamers will see the constructed environments as quite similar to first-person shooters; tabletop role-players will see the Gamemaker role in their own killer game masters.  This may be the first mainstream movie that makes being a game master cool.  And if it gives preteen girls someone to look up to whose goal in life is greater than pining for her vampire boyfriend, I'm okay with that.son looks even more terrified than Lawrence and in a twist plays the \\"damsel in distress\\" to Katniss' vigorous presence; Banks is a cartoonish reminder of what Capitol citizens are like (clowns, basically); Harrelson channels frustrated class rage that keeps him in his spirits until he realizes Katniss has a chance at winning; and finally Kravitz does an excellent job of being the only sane person who seems to care about Katniss as a human being. Add to this combination an actress of the caliber of Charlize Theron and you end up with one disturbing but excellent film.<br /><br />Mavis Gary (Charlize Theron), left her high school popularity in Mercury, MN to move on to Minneapolis to become a famous ghost writer of Young Adult romances.  Now age (she is now 37 years old), bad divorce, writer's block and alcoholism force her to stare in the mirror, see how she has slipped, and respond to an email birth announcement of a child born to her ex-flame Buddy Slade (Patrick Wilson) by deciding to return to Mercury and reignite her old passion for the now happily married Buddy.  She crams her tiny pet dog into her carryon, registers at the local motel (the desk is manned by a shrewd Louisa Krause), and is off to find a drink.  She there encounters Matt Freehauf (Patton Oswalt in a brilliant performance), a high school nobody who suffered massive physical abuse and subsequent permanent damage at the hands of jocks who beat him brutally for being gay (he isn't).  Mavis recognizes Matt, feels sorry for him, and confides in him that she is in town to rekindle her old romance with Buddy.  Despite Matt's warnings that Buddy is off limits Mavis, between bouts of drunken oblivion, fixes her appearance, makes contact with Buddy, is invited to Buddy's home to meet his wife Beth (Elisabeth Reaser) and new daughter, all the while focusing her wiles on seducing Buddy despite all evidence that the affair is dead. How the trip to Mercury ends is the surprising finale.<br /><br />Charlize Theron is such a fine actress that she is able to find crevices where we can peak into her life and find some love for her despicably ruined Mavis. The entire cast is excellent and includes cameos by Mary Beth Hurt and Jill Eikenberry.  Once again Diablo Cody invites us to look at just what defines `adult' and why so many people stumble on their way to that plateau. At times it is difficult to see the comedy in this film, but stand far enough back and it is the human comedy that we are watching.  Cody, with Reitman, makes that clear. B003E6K1VK|5.0|To begin, I can't say much about this in comparison to other DACs or headphone amps. This is my first and only one.<br />Even with that, though, I can say a lot of good about it. I bought these to drive my new Beyerdynamic DT 990 Pros, but I've used them with my old 'phones as well, my Klipsch Image S4s. I wasn't expecting too much with this; namely, enough to drive the headphones. So the first thing that surprised me was the way they were packaged. They came like an iPod, in a robust rectangular box with high quality photographs of the item and the item name and brand printed on the sides. So the good impression starts immediately; this has been packaged quite well. It sits in a plastic basin like an iPod too, but comes in a silicone case, an excellent perk that I wasn't expecting. Then, under the plastic plate was a male to male 3.5mm jack, mini USB cable, manuals, a felt carrying case, and a screen protector. It comes very well-equipped, which was an awesome surprise. FiiO gets a ton of points for that.<br />I was very impressed with the quality of the device itself, too. Taking it out of the silicone case and removing the plastic protector on the front revealed the front to be made of smooth glass and the body to be made of brushed, black metal, with a few bolts on the back holding it together and contributing to the rugged look. The buttons on the side are metal as well. Even if it's not full-on audiophile grade (as other reviewers have said, anyway), it presents itself as a fairly high end item. If you're reluctant to drop the money on something that only drives headphones, the unboxing alone would reassure you a good bit.<br />Since this is all I've used the Beyers on, I can't say much about how far it takes or limits them. What I can say is that, with this driving the Beyerdynamics, I get a very crisp and detailed sound that leaves nothing for me to desire. Someday I may use the cans on a better amp and be able to pick out places where the FiiO lacks, but until then I'd say I feel that I got my money's worth on both products. Since the Beyers are 250 ohm, it's worth saying that the FiiO does drive them very well. I've gotten as loud as I've wanted without hitting max volume and without any apparent changes to the sound profile; everything is very consistent whether it's at 1 or 40.<br />With the Klipschs, I can at least talk about comparison with how they sounded plugged into my iMac (17\\" late 2006) or Thinkpad (X120e). For one thing, the FiiO has considerably less ambient noise. While there is very mild hiss, it's nowhere near as bad as that of the iMac's in-built jack or the unpleasant buzz of the Thinkpad. On the iMac, the S4s sound more detailed and better-imaged through the FiiO than through the headphone jack on the computer. It's an improvement, though with the overall quality of the S4s to begin with it may be slight overkill for them. In total, it ended up costing me something like $95 in contrast to $80 for the buds, and while they do sound better with them, I'd say that if your computer has a decent jack to begin with you'd probably be better off with a ~$150 pair and no DAC. This will vary depending on your computer and headphone selection, but there you go. With the incredible sensitivity of the Klipsch in-ears, you'll also run it at incredibly low volume levels. Right now, I'm listening to \\"Nachuraru-ni Koishite\\" by Perfume on the Klipschs at a mere volume of 3 and that's quite enough.<br />To conclude, though, this is a great little device. For the money, FiiO seems to treat you very well, and I'd certainly buy from them again. Go for it!

1451627289|5.0|For a long time I've wondered if Stephen King would ever come out with a book again that rivaled those years when he had hit after hit and each book was better than the last.  He has achieved that and more with 11/22/63.<br /><br />The main character is Jake Epping, a thirty-something english teacher, who innocently becomes caught up in a time bubble that allows him to travel from 2011 back to 1958 and back again, each trip, no longer how long he stays in 1958 causes only 2 minutes to go by in 2011.  When his friend Al, the local diner owner shows him this, he can't believe it and they start to wonder if they can go back to change some things and right some wrongs.  Jake wants to see if the janitor who is taking his night class could be healed from a life long injury brought on by an abusive father who kills his entire family.  He also goes back to see if he can save a young woman who is wheel chair bound after she is hit with a bullet in a hunting accident.  Things seem to be going well, and he starts to spend more and more time in 1958 where he takes on the name George Amberson.  As George, he teaches school, falls in love with the young librarian and gets enmeshed in the world of 1958, long before he was born.  As he and Al talk he finds that Al's one last wish is that he try to get to 1963 and prevent the Kennedy assasination.  This becomes his focus on the last half of the book, tracking Oswald and his family and trying to figure out how and when he will take the would-be assasin out so that Kennedy can be saved.  But what is the cost?<br /><br />The last quarter of the book is very fast paced and it was difficult to put it down.  You aren't sure which case you will end up with, a John Kennedy who lives and what that would mean to 2011 or a John Kennedy that dies, and if that is the case, why would that be the outcome that we would want?  If he changes the past, will he end up with Sadie, the lovely librarian, or not?<br /><br />As many who read King know, he sometimes can write a long book that you love until the ending comes and you feel like \\"damn, he got me again\\", a great book with an ending that did not satisfy.  This book was not like that in the least.  The ending was fitting, made sense and was extremely satisfying on every level.<br /><br />I would have to say this has been one of the best books I have read in a while and I'm happy to say has that same old vintage King feel that I so loved in the past.<br /><br />I would wholeheartedly recommend this as a great read for those that love King's earlier works, those who love the time travel element or just want a book that is both unique and satisfying.

B004TRKH9O|5.0|Perhaps no other album better defines the aims and techniques of kosmische musik than Zeit. As Julian Cope explains in his Krautrocksampler, \\"...its remarkable unchanging unfolding near-static barely-shifting vegetable organic-ness takes over the room and permeates the whole house...the room in which Zeit is playing becomes that room.\\" To say that Zeit is otherworldly is understating the matter considerably. These are the sounds the stars make as they exhaust their nuclear fuel and collapse inward through the fabric of space itself to some inscrutable fate. These are sonic portraits of yawning alien vistas, hymns to the staggering distances between the island galaxies. Zeit evokes ancient memories of our extraterrestrial origins, visions that are at once immense, incomprehensible, profound, amorphous and utterly cosmic. The sustained cello note that slowly emerges out of the silence at the beginning of \\"Birth of Liquid Plejades\\" resonates like some fearful cord of memory being struck at the base of one's brain, dredging up dim shadows of mystical transfigurations from the inorganic to the sentient, from nonexistence to being. Like an iceberg slowly forming, the piece adds layer upon layer of sound, eventually drifting into an interlude of stasis and calm supported by Edgar Froese's plaintive organ and the aqueous flow of guest Florian Fricke's modular Moog. \\"Nebulous Dawn\\" ushers in Eno's ambient music program a decade ahead of schedule. Rising out of a primordial drone, the piece quickly turns into a lysergic nightmare, visualized in sound and transmitted on the radio waves of the subconscious mind. Its detuned bell tones klang ominously atop a background of carefully mixed and modulated electronic sounds, ranging from high-pitched shrieks and sub-harmonnic rumblings to disembodied moans and the spectral effluvia of ectoplasmic generators. Both \\"Origin of Supernatural Probabilities\\" and \\"Zeit\\" are similar in design, though more minimalist in execution. Quiet and introspective, both of these tracks utilize primarily the same sound sources from \\"Birth of Liquid Plejades\\" and \\"Nebulous Dawn,\\" yet achieve a more muted, autumnal effect. \\"Origin...\\" and \\"Zeit\\" take on the character of phantom landscapes lost somewhere on the border between consciousness and unconsciousness. Listen to Zeit one evening when you're taking a dip in the isolation tank, and you may never find your way back to reality.trospective, both of these tracks utilize primarily the same sound sources from \\"Birth of Liquid Plejades\\" and \\"Nebulous Dawn,\\" yet achieve a more muted, autumnal effect. \\"Origin...\\" and \\"Zeit\\" take on the character of phantom landscapes lost somewhere on the border between consciousness and unconsciousness. But reading the book first is highly recommended.<br /><br />If you take the reality show \\"Survivor\\" and re-imagine it in ancient Rome, you might get the idea behind \\"The Hunger Games\\", a televised contest where 24 teenagers compete to be the last one standing. There's one catch, however. They earn that victory by hunting down and murdering the others. The story take place in a futuristic dystopia called Panem where the central authority, \\"The Capital\\" demands that the twelve formerly rebellious outlying districts send two \\"tributes\\"; kids between ages 12-18, to battle in the Games. All are picked by lottery. Once chosen, they are pampered like modern day reality stars before being sent into the forest with whatever weapons and supplies they can muster.<br /><br />The story focuses on the brave and resourceful Katniss Everdeen (played by the extraordinary Jennifer Lawrence), who volunteers to take the place of her younger sister. The first few scenes are excellent and chilling. Katniss's home, District 12, is an impoverished coal mining backwater that eerily mimics the Appalachian locale of Lawrence's last film, the equally bleak \\"Winter's Bone\\". The children are chosen at a staged event, called a \\"reaping\\", at the train station in a vignette that echoes (deliberately, I assume) the Selection Platform processing at Auschwitz.<br /><br />Once at the Capital, the story sags somewhat. This is supposedly where the rich, venal rulers congregate but, adorned as they are with exaggerated costumes and grotesque florescent hair styles, they look more like extras from a Fellini film. They come off as silly, not threatening, especially Stanley Tucci as the unctuous Ryan Seacrest-like MC who is cursed with a blue-tinted pompadour. The entire sequence is meant to mock the celebrity/reality show culture of our present day but it comes off a bit forced. In the book, they look as bizarre but their malevolence is more apparent. The only character who radiates menace is Donald Sutherland's President Snow and he is dressed in plain formal attire.<br /><br />The story picks up again when the youngsters begin to battle although it's unsettling to see children casually slaughtering each other. Katniss is paired with Peeta (Josh Hutcherson in a tender, underplayed role), a boy from her home district and their love story, ruthlessly exploited by the show's controllers, provides the backdrop for what transpires.<br /><br />\\"The Hunger Games\\", the beginning of the trilogy by author Suzanne Collins (who also wrote the screenplay), promises future sequels which will hopefully give more back story to Panem's beginnings and more challenges for the riveting and poignant Katniss Everdeen. These stories are aimed at teenage readers and it is easy to see their appeal. Combining elements of love and heroism, it also plays out every teen's revenge fantasy of getting back at the creeps who ignore them in the cafeteria and bully them in the hallways. 0312641893|4.0|Uh, dystopian half-metallic Cinderella. Rock on! What a creative story. Marissa Meyer really took this retelling to a different level. This world-building has so many awesomely original aspects that I don't know where to start. Cinder is a part-metal orphan owned by the widow of the man who adopted her, who, of course treats her like a slave. She's a mechanic, lives in New Beijing (old one has fallen) living under an Emperor ruling, who is fighting off war threats from Luna (yes, the moon!) while he struggles to find a cure for the plague that's been killing off the population.There's more! Luna-born people have magical abilities like glamors and mind-controlling!<br /><br />So much to take in, right? Yet Meyer does it in such a smooth way that everything just clicks and, weirdly, even makes sense. She even stays very glued to her Cinderella concept, which I was so immersed in the story that I sometimes forgot. Characters were well developed and very likeable. I loved Cinder's ability to be quite normal under such awful circumstances and still manage to be somewhat of a strong heroine.<br /><br />I also loved how modern social issues such as immigrants, difference, acceptance, politics and so on, where neatly woven into the story as well. It marvels me how much stuff Meyer was able to put into this tale without making it sound awkward or too much. This is a fully-enjoyable, wildly creative, feel-good retelling of the fairytale we all love. Part dystopia, part fantasy, part sci-fi, part romance, this is a book for everyone.
